Sin embargo, con el paso del tiempo, estos sistemas fueron reemplazados por los sistemas de compresi\u00f3n de vapor, gracias al auge de la energ\u00eda el\u00e9ctrica (EE) (2). Lo anterior es una desventaja para las zonas no interconectadas (ZNI), ya que no pueden refrigerar debido a que no tienen un SR que puedan operar con fuentes alternativas de energ\u00eda. Las ZNI corresponden al 52% del territorio Colombiano y alrededor de 1.500.000 personas que no tienen acceso a electricidad (3). Esto conduce a una disminuci\u00f3n en la calidad de vida de las personas que viven en estas \u00e1reas en comparaci\u00f3n con aquellos que viven en \u00e1reas con acceso a la red el\u00e9ctrica. Mediante el desarrollo de este proyecto se busca dise\u00f1ar, construir y poner en funcionamiento un equipo de refrigeraci\u00f3n por absorci\u00f3n a escala laboratorio, que permita determinar la capacidad y eficiencia de refrigeraci\u00f3n. Dicho equipo ser\u00eda un prototipo que luego permitir\u00eda escalar a un nivel superior hasta llegar finalmente a una escala industrial. El prototipo que se desarrolle ser\u00e1 empleado para las asignaturas termodin\u00e1mica, ciclos termodin\u00e1micos y plantas t\u00e9rmicas en el Programa de Ingenier\u00eda Mec\u00e1nica. De esta manera el Semillero seguir\u00e1 enriqueciendo el Laboratorio de Energ\u00edas de la Universidad con dispositivos desarrollados en la misma Universidad. Se espera que en el mediano plazo la Universidad pueda contar con un prototipo que pueda ofertarse a quienes hoy viven en ZNI.\n\nTuberia: cobre, aluminio y acero inoxidable (diferentes diametros) $1\u2019000.000\nLamina: acero inoxidable, acero inoxidable reflectivo      $ 800.000\nUnidad de enfriamiento: Unidad de enfriamiento absorci\u00f3n-difusion 40L $ 1\u2019200.000\nRefrigerante: carga R717 (amoniaco), carga hidrogeno  $300.000\nAislamiento: polipropileno $ 200.000\nInstrumentos: termocuplas, manometros, HVAC&R manifold $500.000\nManufactura: mano de obra de lasoperaciones que no se pueden realizar en la universidad. $ 800.000\nOtros: papeler\u00eda y afines. $ 200.000\nTOtal: $ 5\u2019000.000\n\n\nReferencias:\n1. It is the enclave of an old Chinese settlement going back 200 years amidst the Bidayuh community who built their longhouses around the 1,646ft high Gunung Serembu mountain complex at Bung Muan. Siniawan was made famous by Rajah James Brooke who built a cottage high up in the hills, and within 30 years had attracted world-famous 19th century personalities to Sarawak.\nSir Hugh Low became the first European to travel to Upper Sarawak to explore the region.\nOthers who visited Rajah James Brooke\u2019s Mountain eyrie which received International acclaim were Governor-General of Labuan Sir Spenser Buckingham St John and author of \u201cLife in the Forests in the Far East\u201d.\nAustrian author Madam Ida Laura Pfeiffer, Italian naturalist Odoardo Beccari, British admiral Sir Henry Keppel and world-renowned Alfred Russel Wallace, who was described as one of the greatest scientists of all time. His paper was jointly published with some of Charles Darwin\u2019s writings of the \u201cEvolution of Man\u201d in 1858.\nJames Brooke first travelled up the Sarawak River in 1839 to quell a rebellion led by the Sarawak Malay chief Datu Patinggi Ali who had challenged the authority of the Brunei Sultanate who had colonised the State from as early as the 15th century.\nIn 184, Brooke became their Rajah and their Bidayuh allies at Bung Muan. He also extended the protection of the British navy to the region.\nOn July 15, 1843 a young mid shipman, artist-cum-author Frank Marryat from H.M.S. Samarang was sent on an expedition to the antimony and gold mines in Bau when he wrote:\n\u201cA short distance inland is a mountain, called Sarambo (Serembu), which it was proposed to ascend, as, by our telescopes, we could perceive houses near to its summit and were told it was the residence of some of the mountain Dayaks under Mr Brooke\u2019s sway.\u201d\nWhat Marryat saw was \u201cBung Muan\u201d where a cluster of three villages \u2014 Penijau, Bumbok and Serembu Bidayuh longhouses community.\nThe Bidayuh had chosen to live 1,000 feet high up in the mountains to protect themselves from raids by Sea Dayak pirates during the reign of the Brunei sultanate.\nIn one raid alone in 1838, the \u201cheadhunters\u201d from Skrang and Saribas had pillaged a neighbouring longhouse community several miles upriver at Bung Bratak, killing hundreds and taking many slaves.\nBrooke put an end to head-hunting and built a cottage just above Bung Muan at place called \u201cPeninjau\u201d.\nSpenser St John said Peninjau got its name from a large rock jutting out of mountain from where the Rajah would sit enjoy a panoramic view of Gunung Santubong and the meandering Sarawak River.\nIn November 1854, Brooke met Alfred Wallace in Singapore and invited him to enjoy at his idyllic cottage where the latter spent four months.\nWallace returned to Peninjau in December 1855, with a Malay boy called Ali which by that time it had been developed into a quaint cottage and describes it as a rude wooden lodge where a cool spring under an overhanging rock.\nItalian naturalist Beccari who stayed at Peninjau in 1865 said Brooke\u2019s bungalow was 300 feet above the longhouses at Bung Muan which was describes as \u201ca place with an extensive view\u201d. Beccari in \u201cWanderings in the Great Forests of Borneo\u201d (1865) said a \u201cstream gushed out of a great cave where the rajah\u2019s men would wash and bathe.\u201d\nAfter James Brooke\u2019s demise in 1868, and with his nephew Charles at the helm, \u201cPeninjau\u201d fell into disrepair.\nIn 1912, J.C. Moulton, spent a fortnight at Serembu using the longer but flatter \u201cWestern Paku-Merembeh Route\u201d as a homage to Wallace. Accompanied by American Harrison W Smith who took photographs, they noted there were just remnants of the \u201cbelian\u201d (ironwood) posts left from the Rajah\u2019s time. Sir James Brooke had a bungalow which they helped to build\u201d.\nBrook paid the Peninjau Dayaks one \u201cBedil\u201d (small cannon) as the fee to build his bungalow. Moulton and Smith returned to Kuching using the Peninjau side to Siniawan, which was shorter route home but with a more difficult descent down the steep mountain.\nRajah Brooke\u2019s cottage at \u201cPeninjau\u201d which in Malay means \u201cthe lookout\u201d, remained forgotten until the 1980s when an English Earl Lord Cranbrook, took an interest in this famous location where Wallace had carried out his studies on the Orang Utan. Between 1854 and 1962 Wallace collected an estimated 5,000 new species (mostly animals) during his eight-year tour of the \u2018Malay Archipelago\u2019.\nIt was Wallace who \u2018discovered\u2019 a rare Sarawak\u2019s two-clubbed spider (Friula wallacei) which has been preserved in the Oxford Museum of Natural History in UK. Virtual archaeology applies methods from virtual reality to recreate digital models of past landscapes with human settlements. Immersive applications allow the user to not only see reconstructed architecture from far away, but also to enter the recreated landscape in the first-person perspective and walk around and explore the site.\nHowever, a proper simulation of any virtual landscape should also include the upper half, the sky dome with daylight and an accurately placed sun which can play a role for the simulation of light-and-shadow effects or epiphanies in sacred places, or for proper simulation of the nocturnal appearance, moon, stars and planets, the Milky Way and occasionally the Zodiacal light, thus recreating the complete historical skyscape (Zotti 2015).\nStarting in 2010, the author has been improving the popular open-source desktop planetarium Stellarium to create the most versatile environment for historically accurate skyscape simulation, including the possibility of loading a 3D virtual model of a properly georeferenced landscape with reconstructions of buildings under the artificial sky (Zotti 2016). Moving through the architecture, the user can explore sight lines or temple axes and their possible targets (e.g., solstice sunrises) on the landscape horizon (Frischer et al., 2016), and light and shadow phenomena can be explored with the light of sun, moon or even the planet Venus. The presentation will show Stellarium in use as a simulation environment for both research and outreach to a wide audience.\nReferences:\nDenard, Hugh, 2009. We studied the variability in this sensitivity in promoters primarily recognized by RNAP holoenzymes carrying \u03c370 . From qPCR and live single-cell, single-RNA measurements of the transcription kinetics of several \u03c370-dependent promoters in various conditions and from the analysis of \u03c3 factors population-dependent models of transcription initiation, we \ufb01nd that, the smaller is the time-scale of the closed complex formation relative to the open complex formation, the weaker is a promoter's responsiveness to changes in \u03c338 numbers. This results in grid-like fiber orientation data on all four board surfaces. A fully automated approach [1] is used to reconstruct the knot morphologies based on this data. This figure shows the identification of knot areas and subsequent division into knot sections after exclusion of insignificant small knots. The knots themselves are then modeled as rotationally symmetric cones. Their axes are found by iteratively connecting knot surfaces and\nminimizing previously defined knot measures. A simulated-annealing optimization scheme is employed to minimize the reconstruction error.\nThe reconstructed knot morphologies can now be used to calculate the local orthotropic material directions in any point of the board [2]. Following the so-called grain flow analogy, the longitudinal-tangential fiber directions can be determined by mimicking the trajectories of a laminar fluid flowing along the board axis, with knots as elliptical obstacles. The angle in radial-longitudinal direction is obtained from polynomials fitted to photographs of knot sections.\n\n[1] Kandler, G., Lukacevic, M., & F\u00fcssl, J. (2016). "10.1016/j.is.2016.09.006"}, {"q": "10.1016/j.is.2016.09.006", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT26M42S"}, "id": "l7JGF-SARzE", "snippet": {"defaultAudioLanguage": "en", "channelTitle": "Damisa Kaminsin", "channelId": "UCMGZJqD9_Xzhix-je4oK7Hw", "description": "**Seminar in Zoology II**\n\nTitle: Hard and soft allopatry: modes of geographic speciation of the yellow-bellied kingsnake in North America\n\nMiss Damisa Kaminsin ID: 6270137823\nAdvisor: Asst. Prof. Dr. Amporn Wiwegweaw\n\nAbstract\nThe Mississippi River is recognized as an important geographic barrier to gene flow and generates species in North America. The yellow-bellied kingsnake, Lampropeltis calligaster, is widely distributed in several areas in North America. It consists of three subspecies: L. c. calligaster, L. c. rhombomaculata and L. c. occipitolineata, which differ morphologically and geographically (distinct ecological niches). The former two subspecies are primarily found in western (grasslands) and eastern (forests) sides of the Mississippi River regions, respectively, and they were historically isolated by this river, while the third is restricted to South Florida peninsular. In this study, the researchers utilized phylogeographic analyses to examine timing and mode of speciation and causes of current lineage diversification in the L. calligaster complex. Gene trees constructed by mtDNA (Cytochrome b) and nDNA (CLLAT, NT3, PRLR and VIM56 loci) sequences demonstrated that 98% of individuals were clearly grouped into 3 lineages: western, eastern and South Florida lineages. Divergence dating and historical demography analyses revealed that three subspecies originated during the Pleistocene epoch, followed by range expansion and increasing population size over the last 100,000 years. Additionally, the result from a Mantel test showed that the pattern of lineage diversification is strongly associated with ecological divergence occurred on each side of the river. This result supports the hypothesis that ecological transition due to climate change during the Pleistocene is a causal factor driving L. calligaster diversification. Migrate tests detected symmetric gene flow with equal rate of migration between eastern and western populations, indicating that speciation and current diversification in this species groups is likely due to the effect of ecological transition rather than historical riverine vicariance. Moreover, ecological niche modeling predicted that the future ranges of eastern and South Florida kingsnake may be decreased due to urbanization and climate change. This prediction provides preliminary information to assist conservation planning and management for L. calligaster. in the future. \n\nReferences:\nMcKelvy, A.D., Burbrink, F.T. 2017. Yet, policy debate about its benefits and costs (e.g. (Di Minin et al. 2016 Trends Ecol. Evol. 31, 99\u2013102. (doi:10.1016/j.tree.2015. 12.006); Ripple et al. 2016 Trends Ecol. Evol. 31, 495\u2013496. (doi:10.1016/j. tree.2016.03.011)) focuses only on the hunted species and biodiversity, not the unique behaviour of hunters. Some contemporary recreational hunters from the developed world behave curiously, commonly targeting \u2018trophies\u2019: individuals within populations with large body or ornament size, as well as rare and/or inedible species, like carnivores (Darimont et al. 2015 Science 349, 858\u2013860. (doi:10.1126/science.aac4249)). Although contemporary hunters have been classified according to implied motivation (i.e. for meat, recreation, trophy, or population control; (Festa-Bianchet 2003 Animal Behavior and Wildlife Conservation (eds M Festa-Bianchet, M Apollonio), pp. 191\u2013 207); (Mysterud 2011 J. Appl. Ecol. 48, 827\u2013834. (doi:10.1111/j.1365-2664. 2011.02006.x))) as well the \u2018multiple satisfactions\u2019 they seek while hunting affiliation, appreciation, achievement; (Hendee 1974 Wildl. Soc. Bull. 2, 104\u2013113)), an evolutionary explanation of the motivation underlying trophy hunting has never been pursued. Too costly (difficult, dangerous) a behaviour to be common among other vertebrate predators, we postulate that trophy hunting is in fact motivated by the costs hunters accept. The Healthpraxis", "channelId": "UCrTsVhO4Z5nHa5hfFPHFyQQ", "description": "New Study Shows How Forward Head Posture Affects the Brain. \n\nhttps://spinalresearch.com.au/new-study-shows-how-forward-head-posture-affects-the-brain/ \n\nThanks to Clare McIvor at  February 11, 2020\n\n\u201cIn a neutral head position (with the lateral posture line running straight through the earlobes and shoulders, with shoulder blades retracted), the average human head weighs 10-12 pounds (4.5-5.5 Kilograms). However, as the head moves forward, this number increases drastically.\n\nAt 15 degrees, the head weighs 27 pounds (12.3 kilograms)\nAt 30 degrees, it increases to 40 pounds (18.2 kilograms)\nAt 45 degrees, it weighs 49 pounds (22.3 kilograms)\nAt 60 degrees, it exerts a force of 60 pounds on the cervical spine. (27.3 kilograms)\nAt 90 degrees, the force could not be measured.\n\nREFERENCES:\n\nIbrahim M Moustafa, Ahmed Youssef, Amal Ahbouch, May Tamim, Deed E. The study suggests populations that led to the lineage split were older than previously thought. \nhttp://www.cam.ac.uk/research/news/virtual-fossil-reveals-last-common-ancestor-of-humans-and-neanderthals\n\nReference\nVirtual ancestor reconstruction: Revealing the ancestor of modern humans and Neandertals\nJournal of Human Evolution Volume 91, February 2016, Pages 57\u201372, Doi:10.1016/j.jhevol.2015.11.002\nhttp://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0047248415002729\nPDF: https://www.academia.edu/19891591/Virtual_ancestor_reconstruction_Revealing_the_ancestor_of_modern_humans_and_Neandertals?auto=download\n\nAbstract\nThe timing and geographic origin of the common ancestor of modern humans and Neandertals remain controversial. A poor Pleistocene hominin fossil record and the evolutionary complexities introduced by dispersals and regionalisation of lineages have fuelled taxonomic uncertainty, while new ancient genomic data have raised completely new questions. Here, we use maximum likelihood and 3D geometric morphometric methods to predict possible morphologies of the last common ancestor of modern humans and Neandertals from a simplified, fully resolved phylogeny. We describe the fully rendered 3D shapes of the predicted ancestors of humans and Neandertals, and assess their similarity to individual fossils or populations of fossils of Pleistocene age. Our results support models of an Afro-European ancestral population in the Middle Pleistocene (Homo heidelbergensis sensu lato) and further predict an African origin for this ancestral population.", "title": "Virtual Fossil: reconstruction reveals last common ancestor of humans and Neanderthals", "publishedAt": "2016-04-29T17:54:09Z"}, "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "719", "dislikeCount": "1", "likeCount": "3"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.is.2015.11.002"}, {"q": "10.1016/j.is.2015.11.002", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT11M23S"}, "id": "6j25WDuqMmM", "snippet": {"tags": ["propaganda model", "media ownership", "noam chomsky", "mr tweedy", "propaganda filter", "media ownership and control", "media ownership documentary", "propaganda model noam chomsky", "propaganda model of communication", "propaganda model theory", "propaganda model herman and chomsky", "propaganda model chomsky", "manufacturing consent", "mr tweedy documentaries"], "channelTitle": "Mr Tweedy Documentaries", "channelId": "UCmjKcF2Cl1kuKgoitas0cjA", "description": "Noam Chomsky's 1st of 5 manufacture of consent media filters. link:\n\nhttps://doi-org.proxy.lib.sfu.ca/10.1016/j.bushor.2016.11.002\n\nTapping into the creativity of a crowd can provide a highly efficient and effective means of acquiring ideas, work, and content to solve problems. But crowdsourcing solutions can also come with risks, including the legal risks associated with intellectual property. Therefore, we raise and address a two-part question: Why\u2013\u2014and how\u2013\u2014should organizations deal with intellectual property issues when engaging in the crowdsourcing of solutions? The answers lie in understanding the approaches for acquiring sufficient intellectual property from a crowd and limiting the risks of using that intellectual property. Herein, we discuss the hazards of not considering these legal issues and explain how managers can use appropriate terms and conditions to balance and mitigate the risks associated with soliciting solutions from a crowd. Based on differences in how organizations acquire intellectual property and limit associated risks, we identify and illustrate with examples four approaches for managing intellectual property (passive, possessive, persuasive, and prudent) when crowdsourcing solutions. In mid-2013 this species was first discovered (and found to be widely distributed) on coral reefs in the southwest corner of the Gulf of Mexico (GoMx), adjacent to a large offshore oilfield. Prior to the present report this species had not been found outside the GoMx. This video documents the finding of that species at Trinidad, 3,300 km from the GoMx, at another offshore oilfield. As these two distant locations are the only west Atlantic sites N. cyanomos currently is known to occur the Trinidad finding supports the hypothesis that it was transported to tropical Atlantic oilfields by oil industry infrastructure being relocated from the Indo-Pacific.\n\nPublished August 9, 2019 on Zenodo repository https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.3364568\n\nBelow are links to information cited in the video.\n1. http://dx.doi.org/10.3391/bir.2014.3.1.09 \n2. http://dx.doi.org/10.3897/zookeys.640.10862\n3. https://youtu.be/VVlg6iakOXw\n4. https://zenodo.org/record/58455\n5. https://nas.er.usgs.gov/queries/FactSheet.aspx?SpeciesID=2936, \n6. https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.4328 \n7. "7QdENQLx8FE", "snippet": {"tags": ["archaeology"], "channelTitle": "Recording Archaeology", "channelId": "UC08QKQO1qs6OPQs9l1kMQPg", "description": "Computer-based visualization has become a powerful tool for both research on cultural heritage artefacts and dissemination of research results to a broader audience (Denard 2009). Virtual archaeology applies methods from virtual reality to recreate digital models of past landscapes with human settlements. Immersive applications allow the user to not only see reconstructed architecture from far away, but also to enter the recreated landscape in the first-person perspective and walk around and explore the site.\nHowever, a proper simulation of any virtual landscape should also include the upper half, the sky dome with daylight and an accurately placed sun which can play a role for the simulation of light-and-shadow effects or epiphanies in sacred places, or for proper simulation of the nocturnal appearance, moon, stars and planets, the Milky Way and occasionally the Zodiacal light, thus recreating the complete historical skyscape (Zotti 2015).\nStarting in 2010, the author has been improving the popular open-source desktop planetarium Stellarium to create the most versatile environment for historically accurate skyscape simulation, including the possibility of loading a 3D virtual model of a properly georeferenced landscape with reconstructions of buildings under the artificial sky (Zotti 2016). Moving through the architecture, the user can explore sight lines or temple axes and their possible targets (e.g., solstice sunrises) on the landscape horizon (Frischer et al., 2016), and light and shadow phenomena can be explored with the light of sun, moon or even the planet Venus. The presentation will show Stellarium in use as a simulation environment for both research and outreach to a wide audience.\nReferences:\nDenard, Hugh, 2009. You may have heard that the eyes are the window to the brain. This is because your eyes are direct extensions of your brain that sprout out during development. Then, we rely on our visual system to tell us where we are in space and what is around us. In order to map our world, we need the ability to move our eyes to see it. This requires a large amount of brain activity from many areas that are interconnected through brain circuits. When one part of the brain is not functioning well, we can identify and rehabilitate that area via eye movements. First, I need to introduce what the term Nyonya means! Basically, Nyonya cuisine originates from the Peranakans, descendants of early Chinese migrants who went to Malaysia, and married local Malay women over 500 years ago. They were also known as the Straits Chinese, who settled in Malacca, Penang and Singapore. Female Peranakans are known as nyonyas, while men are called babas. Basically, my ancestors hopped on a boat from the Fujian Province in China, in search of a better life and landed in a small coastal town in Malaysia called Malacca. I am a sixth generation Straits born Chinese.\n\nThe language of the Peranakans, Baba Malay, is a dialect of the Malay language, containing many Hokkien words. Its use is mainly limited to the older generation. However, English has replaced baba malay as the main language spoken amongst the younger generation. Therefore, I cannot speak any Chinese (even though I look ethnically Chinese), and have been called a banana (yellow on the outside and white on the inside!). The Malay phrase \"Orang Cina Bukan Cina\", which literal translation means, \"Chinese but not Chinese\" captures the relationship between the Peranakan and Chinese identity.\n\nIn the past, nyonya girls were not educated in schools and were trained to be amazing cooks and skilled in embroidery from young. They were expected to look after the home and prepare for marriage. In those days, matchmakers played an important role. They would arrive the home at 10 am, to listen to the sound of spices being pounded by the prospective bride, in the mortar and pestle (batu lesong/tumbuk). Apparently, one would be able to tell if the person was a good cook by the rhythm and sound of the pounding. I am so thankful I was not born during that time as I would fail miserably! Today, we also simplify our lives by using a food processor to blend all our spices.\n\nNyonya food is complicated and can take years to master. Nyonya recipes are also handed down from one generation to the next. My mom does not use written recipes with measurements. All ingredients are measured via estimates (agak agak). Basically, you had to learn how to cook by observing and practicing. It\u2019s only recently that I have asked my mom to quantify ingredients (in which she just replies with \u201cone or two tablespoons\u201d or a \u201chandful\u201d. I literally bring out my weighing scale or measuring spoons to measure the ingredients when she puts it aside!\n\nSignature ingredients in nyonya recipes are usually spicy, and use numerous pungent roots such as galangal, turmeric and ginger; aromatic leaves (kaffir lime, pandan, curry and laksa leaves); and other ingredients such as shallots, lemongrass, shrimp paste (belacan) and chillies. There are many other spices that I do not cover in this video, namely, torch ginger buds (bunga kantan), candle nuts (buah keras), buah keluak, tamarind (asam jawa), coriander seeds (ketumbar), betel leaves (daun kaduk),  galangal leaves, and coriander leaves, which are also common in nyonya cooking.\n\nThese spices and herbs are common in Asia, but quite expensive in Australia. Hence, many of these ingredients are imported frozen from Vietnam and available in the Asian grocery store. I personally freeze all my herbs and spices in Melbourne (e.g., lemongrass, pandan leaves, kaffir lime leaves, chilli, ginger, galangal, turmeric) so that I have stock at home all the time! I also use readily frozen shredded lemongrass and galangal in my cooking (think of time saving)! When chillies are cheap, I buy it to blend and freeze.\n\nOverall, Peranakan or Nyonya cuisine is extremely popular in Asia. If you haven't tried this cuisine, I encourage you to visit a Nyonya restaurant the next time you visit Malacca, Penang or Singapore. The food is truly a unique blend of Malay, Chinese and other Southeast Asian cultures!\n\nReferences: \nLee, C.K. (1974). Mrs. Lee's cookbook: Nonya recipes and other favourite recipes. Eurasia Press. Singapore\n\nNg, C.Y. & Karim, S.A. (2016). Historical and contemporary perspectives of the Nyonya food culture in Malaysia. Journal of Ethnic Foods. All common mobile platforms and many desktop systems now support emoji entry, and users have embraced their use. Yet, we currently know very little about what makes for good emoji entry. While soft keyboards for text entry are well optimized, based on language and touch models, no such information exists to guide the design of emoji keyboards. In this article, we investigate of the problem of emoji entry, starting with a study of the current state of the emoji keyboard implementation in Android. To enable moving forward to novel emoji keyboard designs, we then explore a model for emoji similarity that is able to inform such designs. This semantic model is based on data from 21 million collected tweets containing emoji. We compare this model against a solely description-based model of emoji in a crowdsourced study. Our model shows good perfor mance in capturing detailed relationships between emoji.\n\nDOI:: https://doi.org/10.1145/3039685\nWEB: https://uist.acm.org/uist2017/\n\nRecorded at the ACM Symposium on As such, QSMs enable computation of detailed tree properties that have been laborious or impossible to measure before. The computed tree properties can be used as classification features for tree species recognition.\n\nThe first half of this video illustrates how we define the 15 classification features our research group has used for a species recognition study. An example QSM is used to visualize the relevant tree parts and key steps in the feature computations.\n\nThe second half shows how the feature values of over a thousand Finnish trees of three different species, Silver birch, Scots pine and Norway Spruce, are distributed, and how well the species separate in the defined feature dimensions. Prenatal yoga doesn\u2019t have to be all downface dogs and worrying about controlling farts. What if I told you yoga can be as simple as just moving your lips?\nThe legendary Ina May Gaskin recommends all birthing women practice using \u201chorse lips\u201d. It\u2019s really as simple as blowing raspberries. Apart from the fact that it relieves tension, and can bring some humour to an intense situation, a relaxed jaw means a relaxed pelvis and more elastic cervix. As I mentioned in my first video, tension in your mouth and jaw correlates to tension in your pelvic region. A relaxed jaw and throat during labour and birth can reduce the need for stitches (Gaskin, p. 178). \nA recent randomized control trial found that women who completed prenatal yoga reported reduced pain labor, less inductions and improved outcomes following childbirth. \nYoga teaching tie in so well with positive psychology, mindfulness and childbirth education. I also recommend yoga to all pregnant women because it\u2019s evidence-based. \n3 things yoga directly benefits in pregnancy and labour:\nBreathing\nWomen who regularly practice relaxation breathing are likely to have calmer labours with improved oxygen and blood flow to both mum and baby. The uterus is a complex series of muscles and all muscles need adequeate oxygen flow to work properly.\nIncreased confidence and self-efficacy\nYoga increases self-efficacy from the point of view learning postures, building strength, regulating breathing and the practice of deepening the mind-body connection. In labour women will go into a deep, internal state. Yoga practice allows preparation for entering this state in a way that is gentle and self-directed. \nPositive language\nResearch shows yoga is also good birth preparation because language is positive, gentle and encouraging. Yoga classes for pregnant women allow the creation of safe, women-only groups where anxieties, experiences and stories can be shared, and techniques for coping with pain and anxiety practiced.\n\nReading\nCampbell, V. What is the cause and how is hyptertension treated? \n\nWhat is hypertension?\nThe definition of hypertension is an elevated blood pressure that is higher than a systolic (the top number) 140 over a diastolic value of 90 (the lower number). \nHypertension is caused by environmental factors such as diet, physical factors, toxins, and psychosocial factors.\nHow is hypertension diagnosed?\nTaking blood pressure, manual blood pressure is the most accurate, taken in a Drs surgery.  This would need to be evaluated over a time frame as sometimes people have \u2018white coat syndrome\u2019 where their blood pressure just goes crazy high when they visit their GP or a hospital.  Side note: it is always good to know your blood pressure and what is normal for you.  This is especially important when you go into hospital and it's not in the normal range.\nTreatment?\nYour weight directly correlates with your blood pressure. \nWorldwide obesity rates are increasing \u2013 obesity is pandemic, and this means so is hypertension and the associated risks of cardiovascular disease, diabetes, inflammation and cancer. \nWhat diet you eat (specifically, Dietary Approaches to Stop Hypertension (DASH)-style diets, vegetarian diets, and, to a lesser extent, Mediterranean-style diets), and your weight are the biggest factors that can be modified to reduce your blood pressure, as well as reducing alcohol, smoking and sodium intake.\nIf you are currently on antihypertensives, this does not mean that you must be on medication for life. You can easily modify your lifestyle and diet to reduce your blood pressure. It is estimated that approximately every kilogram you reduce your weight by, so does your systolic blood pressure reading.\nI hope this has been helpful. Take control of your lifestyle if you want to live a long and healthy life.\n\nReferences: \nAppel, L. J. (2017). The Effects of Dietary Factors on Blood Pressure. Of course, Hebbian theory and Kandel\u2019s work can not exist without Pavlov and his dog, which couldn\u2019t exist without Vogt, and the cycle goes on and on! Varistors based on SnO2 exhibit both high nonlinear coefficients and high breakdown electric fields. In this work we present a facile means for controlling the breakdown electric field in the SnO2-CoO-Cr2O3-Nb2O5 varistor system by the introduction of 1D SnO2 nanobelts. The materials were prepared by a solid state reaction method with Nb2O5 doping levels at 0.10 and 0.20, mole percent. The materials were studied in detail by dual beam microscopy, direct current and impedance measurements. Exaggerated three-dimensional growth of the tin dioxide belts was observed, which was attributed to Ostwald ripening. The breakdown electric field was observed to decrease from 2510 V/cm to 2280 V/cm and from 1700 V/cm to 804 V/cm after nanobelt insertion, into the systems with 0.10 and 0.20, mole percent Nb2O5 respectively. A model for the observed results was proposed based on the percolation of electrons through the belts, decreasing the number of effective potential barriers at the grain boundaries. The simulations of the impedance data showed one order of magnitude decrease in the grain boundary resistance due to the nanobelt insertion for both studied systems.\n\nMateus G.Masteghin, Jos\u00e9 A.Varela, Marcelo O.Orlandi\n\nDOI: https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jeurceramsoc.2016.12.018\n\nMore: http://www.cdmf.org.br", "title": "Controlling the breakdown electric field in SnO2 based varistors by the insertion of SnO2 nanobelts", "publishedAt": "2018-04-23T14:42:25Z"}, "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "47", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "0"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.ins.2016.12.018"}, {"q": "10.1016/j.ins.2016.12.018", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT2M4S"}, "id": "Kvh--ydNVR8", "snippet": {"title": "A Greening Earth", "channelTitle": "Snow M.T.", "publishedAt": "2017-05-02T21:25:51Z", "description": "A significant greening Earth was detected by NASA's satellite measurement from 2001 to 2015. Scientists study these signals to find out how they can cause a cell's particular behaviour, and how they can be manipulated with drugs to treat diseases such as cancer. \n\nFor decades, a major way scientists have studied cancer in the lab has been by growing a single layer of cancer cells in a dish, doing experiments on those cells and observing the outcome. Although this approach has taught us an unbelievable amount about how both healthy and diseased cells work, the reality of cells in an environment like the human body is much more complex.\n\nThe microenvironment inside a tumour contains more than just cancer cells. Many different kinds of healthy cells are present in the tumour as well, and can have their healthy function hijacked by the cancer cells. For instance, fibroblasts inside the tumour can be co-opted by the cancer cells in order to grow a support structure for the cancer cells to grow on. There are all kinds of different immune cells inside a tumour as well, which try to attack the tumour but can end up being hijacked themselves as well and end up helping the cancer grow. \n\nInside the microenvironment of the tumour, all these different cell types, both healthy and diseased, are constantly communicating with one another. We call this complex tangle of communication between different types of cells Heterocellular Signalling. We know very little about how this heterocellular signalling works inside a tumour, because it's just been too difficult to study this dynamic behaviour.\n\nBut new technological advances are allowing us to finally figure out how all these different cell types are communicating with one another. This will teach us lots about how a tumour grows and spreads, and will hopefully allow us to find new ways of treating cancer.\n\nFor more information, see:\n- Chris Tape's latest review in Trends in Cancer on heterocellular signalling: http://dx.doi.org/10.1016/j.trecan.2016.12.004\n- His blogpost partnering the review: http://christape.com/blog/2016/12/5/heterocellular-emergence\n- Our video on JETem 2017", "channelTitle": "JETem", "publishedAt": "2017-07-06T23:58:52Z", "description": "Patel A, et al. Pneumonia Diagnosed by Point-of-Care Ultrasound. JETem 2017. 2(3):V11-12. https://doi.org/10.21980/J8XP64\n\nHistory of present illness:  A 46-year-old female was sent to the emergency department from clinic with an oxygen saturation of 86%. She endorsed fevers with cough and generalized weakness for one week. She had been evaluated by her primary care provider on day two of illness and was started empirically on Oseltamivir without improvement of her symptoms. The patient arrived febrile, tachycardic, tachypneic, and hypoxic on room air with left-sided crackles on exam.\n\nSignificant findings: Point-of-care ultrasound of the left lower lobe demonstrates lung hepatization, a classic finding for pneumonia. In addition, a shred sign is present with both air bronchograms and focal B-lines\u2014all suggestive of poorly aerated, consolidated lung(1). The patient was started on antibiotics and admitted to the hospital with a diagnosis of community-acquired pneumonia.\n\nDiscussion: With a systematic approach, a skilled clinician can distinguish patterns on point-of-care ultrasound to evaluate for underlying pathology of the lung. Such findings include focal B-lines, an artifact that results from altered fluid-air compositions in the alveoli(2,3).\n\nStudies show, that when referenced with the gold standard of computed tomography, ultrasound is more sensitive than chest X-ray in diagnosing pneumonia. Nazerian et al(4). conducted a 190-patient study in which ultrasound had a higher sensitivity\u201481% compared to a dismal 65% by chest X-ray. Similarly, Cortellaro et al(5). concluded bedside ultrasound (96% sensitivity) is a reliable and superior tool for timely diagnosis of pneumonia compared to chest X-ray (69% sensitivity).\n\nBourcier et al(6). drew conclusions on the effectiveness of ultrasound over chest X-ray on two groups of patients. In the group who exhibited symptoms for less than 24 hours, ultrasound outperformed chest X-ray, where the PPV of ultrasound was 76% and chest X-ray 23%. For those who exhibited symptoms for more than 24 hours, ultrasound demonstrated a PPV of 93% while chest X-ray only 77%. Thus, the use of point-of-care ultrasound in the diagnosis of pneumonia can improve sensitivity while minimizing radiation. \n\nTopics: Pneumonia, ultrasound, dyspnea, shortness of breath, hypoxic, respiratory, pulmonology. \n\nReferences: \n1. El estudio elegido para divulgar durante el v\u00eddeo nos propone lo siguiente.\n Misma paciente, una mujer de 63 a\u00f1os con historia de dolor y s\u00edntomas radiculares L5, el s\u00edndrome radicular no es m\u00e1s\n que un conjunto de signos y s\u00edntomas causado por una presi\u00f3n ejercida sobre la ra\u00edz de un nervio, en este caso la ra\u00edz L5.\nEsta paciente visit\u00f3 10 centros de imagen diferentes en un periodo de 3 semanas. Los hallazgos de las diferentes resonancias magn\u00e9ticas fueron comparados con un examen de referencia, dos de los autores del estudio revisaron los hallazgos de la resonancia de referencia de forma absolutamente independiente para establecer un diagnostico final como consenso.\n\nLos resultados m\u00e1s importantes son los siguientes:\n- Hubo una variabilidad importante en el informe de hallazgos patologicos de la resonancia magnetica, es decir \nhubo una gran diversidad de diagnosticos diferentes.\n- Hubo un numero elevado de errores de interpretacion. Esto significa que algunas patologias, importantes o no, \nno fueron descritas en sus respectivos informes. Simplemente se entregaron informes en la mayor\u00eda de casos imcompletos.\n- Se describrieron 49 hallazgos patol\u00f3gicos diferentes entre todos los informes de las dferentes resonancias.\nComo por ejemplo, protrusi\u00f3n discal, hernia de disco, compresion de la raiz nerviosa, degeneracion facetaria, estenosis\ncentral, etc...\n- Ninguno de los 49 hallazgos fueron descritos por unanimidad en los 10 informes de resonancia magnetica. Solo uno de ellos\nespondilolistesis anterior, fue informado en 9 de los 10 informes. The scaling exponent represents the fractal degree and can be interpreted as a \u201cbiomarker\u201d of relative diseases. The previous study showed that the average wavelet method provides the most accurate results to estimate this scaling exponent when applied to stride interval time series. The purpose of this paper is to determine the most suitable mother wavelet for the average wavelet method. This paper presents a comparative numerical analysis of 16 mother wavelets using simulated and real fractal signals. Simulated fractal signals were generated under varying signal lengths and scaling exponents that indicate a range of physiologically conceivable fractal signals. The five candidates were chosen due to their good performance on the mean square error test for both short and long signals. Next, we comparatively analyzed these five mother wavelets for physiologically relevant stride time series lengths. Our analysis showed that the symlet 2 mother wavelet provides a low mean square error and low variance for long time intervals and relatively low errors for short signal lengths.
